the Sacramento at

Special Dispatch to The Call.
REDDING, June 21.â A novel method
'. ot gold hunting has Just been Instituted
within the city limits of Redding. Fred
Honle, Charles Weils and Samuel Cul
len, professional divers from Port Hu
â  ron, Mich., have located a claim on the
bottom of the Sacramento River. They
; have a scow anchored In the center of
i the stream. A ladder reaches to the
bottom. The gold hunters Â£o down In
full diving costume. The river is thirty
feet deep at this point and so swift
that the divers have to wear heavy
weights at the waist and on their feet.
< iperations were begun yesterday. A
!:iy' j r of gravel from six inches to sev
eral feet deep was found on the bot
tom. Gold is deposited on the bedrock
. beneath. Yesterday twenty-nine small
pieces of gold were found.
The divers found a large crevice,
which they will thoroughly explore.
They are highly pleased with the pros
, pects. If indications continue ma
chinery working on the suction prin

Big Crop on Flood Land.
STOCKTON. June 21.â One of the most
remarkable wheat crops of the year will
; come from what is known as the Bell-
Fabian tract n"ar Hethoney, in this
county. The land has hardly been free
, from periodical Inundation for twenty
years. Last September there was water
! nil over the section whore the crop is now
1 nonring harvest. After the water receded
j disk harrows were sent and after scratch-
Ing the surface the grain was sowed,
comparatively speaking on the damp sur
i face. All kind? of predictions were made
| about the seed rotting before It could
take root, but instead It sprang up and
from the flrÂ«t advanced more rapidly than
the carefully cultivated crops. It is esti
mated that the 3400 acres will produce
I 40.000 sacks of grain, a fortune to the own
ers for their small outlay and little
trouble.

EVEN WHITE
WOMEN ARE
NOT SAFE
Serious Charge Against Ne
gro Troops Sent to Per
secute Union Miners.
MILITARISM IN IDAHO
Orders of the Secretary of War
Ignored and Prosecution of Labor
Organizations Continues
Special Dispatch to The Call.
The order of Secretary of War
Alger to General Merr/am to permit
subordinate commanders to take
no part in the Coeur d'Alene
troubles other than assist the lo-
cal authorities to preserve the
peace has, apparently, had little
effect upon the military gentlemen
who are overturning the cons/titu
tion in Idaho.
The latest outrage consists in
insults offered by the troopers in
blue to white women, instituting a
reign of such nameless terror as
to make men's blood boil.
WALLACE, Idaho, June 21.â
The prosecution of members
of labor organizations still
continues. Despite the fact
that the Secretary of War
has Issued orders that United States
troops can be used only to suppress
riot the military authorities are doing i
their utmost to drive every sympa
thizer with unionism out of the State. |
The Hunter mine, owned by St. Paul
parties, has been running for several
days with union men. Yesterday the
superintendent was told that if he did
not compel his men to sign the infa
mous permits he would be thrown into
the "bull pen." He defied the military
and declared that he would never ask !
a. man in his employ to sign such a i
document. His incarceration is hourly
expected.
Lieutenant Lyons, who is stationed at j
Burke in command of a company of j
negro soldiers, is especially autocratic.
He not only refuses to allow men to j
seek employment until they secure per
mits, but compels union members of |
the Cooks' Union to prepare meals for
non-union men at the point of the bay
onet. â 
His soldiers are accused of insulting
white women and the community about
Burke is in a state of terror occasioned
by the conduct of his troops. The pro
gramme of the Governor and mine
owners, published nearly a month ago,
is being carried out. \
France, the acting Sheriff and em
ploye of the Bunker Hill Company,
packed the Grand Jury with pronounced
anti-union men. even summoning one
of his own deputies. The indictments
returned by this Grand Jury implicate j
less than one hundred persons, yet over
two hundred men. whose cases were in
vestigated by this fixed Jury and
against whom no indictments were re
turned, are still confined in the bull
pen.
A case of smallpox has developed in
a house not one hundred feet from
where the men are confined, and should
it attack those In the prison the death
rate "would be enormous as there are
no facilities for coping with the disease.
Prisoners in the pen are subjected to
the most inhuman abuse. They are
constantly sworn at and reviled by
thHr negro guards, and the food fur
nished is of a disgusting character. A
diary- kept by one of the prisoners says
that nearly all the moat furnished is
decayed and worm-eaten. The County
Commissioners are still kept in prison,
although no indictments have been
found against them, and Sinclair openly
boasts that he keeps them Imprisoned
simply because he chooses to and has
the power.
The Commissioners are highly re
spected throughout the county and can
prove beyond question that they were
at their homes on the days of the riot.
That the military are being kept here
for the sole purpose of destroying the
miners' unions and intimidating all
persons in sympathy with organized
labor is now not denied, and it is openly
boasted by deputies in the employ of
the mine owners that everything has
been fixed "all the way at present" to
keep the soldiers here as long as the
Governor desires.

CHAMBERS AND KAUTZ ARE
SUSTAINED.
COMMISSION ENDS SAMOAN DISPUTE
APIA, Samoa, June 14 (via Auckland, N. Z., June 21). â Mataafa has surrendered 1850 rifles,
and the loyalists have given up 2000. After June 20 a heavy penalty will be enforced upon
natives found with rifles in their possession. Mataafa promised to turn in more weapons.
The natives have returned to their homes.
Malietoa Tanu was recognized as king by the Commissioners of the three powers, and the
decision of Chief Justice Chambers in the matter of the kingship was proclaimed valid and bind
ing. Malietoa Tanu then abdicated in favor of the Commissioners, who appointed a provisional
government, consisting of the Consuls of the three powers, empowering a majority to act in all
cases where unanimity is not required by the Berlin treaty.
Chief Justice Chambers continues to hold office, and the various municipal officials are con
firmed. Dr. Wilhelm Solf has been authorized to act as president of the municipality of Apia. The
Commissioners expect to leave on June 28, but they have requested Chief Justice Chambers to re
main. The report recommends the abolition of kingship and the presidency of Apia and the ap
pointment of a Governor, with a Legislative Council consisting of three nominees of the inter
ested powers, assisted by a native House.
Under this scheme the Governor would have a veto over general and municipal laws; tfie
nominees would be the department heads; consular, diplomatic and judicial functions would be
abolished: revenue would be raised by an increase of duties and poll tax; the jurisdiction of the
Supreme Court would be increased, the municipality, under a Mayor and council, would be ex
tended, and the postoffice would be under the general government.
The British cruiser Porpoise, Captain Sturdee, left, via Fiji, on June 8.
Apia and the country roundabout are resuming normal conditions. Americans and British
express satisfaction at the decision of the Commissione rs.
Among the Germans it is rumored that Dr. Solf will be the administrator in the new native
government.

As the latter was being marched aboard

hyn up" on the first opportunity. As
soon as Burns was released he secured
a Colt's navy revolver from the armory
by some means, and, walking up be
hind McDell, shot him in the side. The
bullet passed through the corporal's
body, close to the surface, narrowly
missed the spine and came out on the
other side. McDell was carried into the
"sick bay" and Burns was placed In
the "brig." When it was found that
McDell would undoubtedly recover
Burns was court-martialed and : :n
--tenced to five years' imprisonment, to
lose all his back pay and to be dishon
orably discharged from the United
States navy. He will be sent to the
Mare Island naval prison to-morrow.

SAMOAN OUTLOOK NOT
ALTOGETHER SATISFACTORY
I.' Â»NDON, June 22.â The correspond
ent of the Times at Apia. Samoa, under
date of June 13, says: "Although con
siderable success has attended the
labors of the Commissioners, it cannot
lid that the outlook for the im
mediate future is altogether satisfac
tory. lii order t" secure unanimity, the
Commissioners have followed to a peril
ous extent a policy of compromise,
\s:tli the result that many conclusions
at which they arrived are inconsistent
and 'â <=' leasing to all parties.
"The only completely satisfactory
features are the disarmament and dis
bandment of the natives, the resigna
tion of Malietoa Tanu and the abolition
of the kingship. These incidents great
ly relieve the fusion and modify the
fears her outbreak, but the
proclamation of a provisional Govern

FIRE AT MENLO PARK.
Hard Work Aione Prevents an Exten
sive Conflagration.
REDWOOD CITY, June 21.-Menlo
Park was visited by fire this morning,
and but for the prompt action of the
volunteer fire department at that place
half of the town would now be in ashes.
Within the last two years three hotels
there have been burned to the ground,
two of which have been rebuilt, and less
than a month ago Hoitt's School was
destroyed.
The fire to-day started in the barn
connected with Martin Kuck's hotel.
The barn was surrounded by other
buildings, and had they taken fire the
whole block would have gone. The de
partment has only one available fire
hydrant, its new ones, six or seven in
number, not yet having been put into
place. Only lately has there been any
fire department at all, Menlo having
been dependent upon Palo Alto and
Redwood City for assistance.
The citizens have now become thor
oughly alarmed. They had recently
purchased 500 feet of fire hose but at
the time of the fire had not taken it
from the railroad freight house. In or
der to use it to-day they were compelled
to break into the freight shed to get it,
the agent not being there at the time
The fire department at Redwood was
telephoned to, but by the time it
reached the scene the fire was under
control. The barn was a total loss.

He May Secure the Command of the
South. Atlantic or Pacific
Station.
WASHINGTON, June 21.â Should the
President decide, after consultation
with Secretary Long, not to direct any

the suggestion will be advanced to him
that the rear admiral be given duty at
sea. Rear Admiral Sehley stands ready
for sea service, and after his return

mand of a squadron. His application,
however, was not granted, although the
department in the meantime assigned
Rear Admijal Kautz to command the
Pacific Station and organized a South
Atlantic Station with Rear Admiral
Howison as its commander in chief.
Because of the lack of men-of-war the
European squadron has not been re
formed.
Hear Admiral Howison will retire
from active service on October 10, but
will be detached from his command on
OctODer 0, on -rvlik-n dutP thf. Chicago
will arrive in New York. It is sug
gested that Rear Admiral Sehley be as
signed to duty as his successor. It may
be that Admiral Kautz, when he arrives
at San Francisco, will be willing to re
linquish the command of the Pacific
squadron, in which event this duty will
be given to Sehley.
PERMANENT CHALLENGE
CUP FOR AUTOMOBILES
An American Presents a Trophy That
May Be Contested for by
Any Ciub.
Special Cable to The Call and the New York
Herald. Copyr'ghted. 1899, by James Gor
don Bennett.
PARIS, June 21.â An American mem
ber of the Automobile Club de France
has just presented a cup as a permanent
challenge trophy. It is to be called
the Columbia challenge cup. It is given
to the club and can only be raced for by
members of an automobile club in any
country, and the challenge must come
through their club. It will thus occupy
in international automobilism the same
place held by the America's cup in in
ternational yachting.
Races will take place in the country
of the club holding the cup for the
time being. The trophy will be consid
ered as belonging to the club anl not
to the individual member whose auto
mobile wins it.
The first race may take place in Sep
tember, and naturally here in France.
as the cup until won out of its keeping
belongs to the Automobile Club de
France.

NEW HAVEN, Conn., June 21.â Rev.
W. M. Harrow?, thf> minister who re
cently married Perry Belmont and Mrs.
Henry D. Sloan at Greenwich, was to
day severely censured by the general
association of Congregational minis
ters of Connecticut, of which he is a
member. Dr. Barrows was present and
personally apologized to the ministers,
saying that he had been imposed upon
I by Mr. Belmont and Mrs. Sloan; but
i In spite of this, resolutions were passed
i censuring him and forbidding any Con
' gregational minister of the association
; to marry any divorced person who has
: been shown guilty by the decree.
By passing the resolution, the clergy
man present bound themselves to take
a firm stand on the divorce question
and use all their power to bring about
uniform divorce laws.
The resolutions also make it Impossi
ble for thorn to remarry guilty persons
in a divorce and persons who are for
bidden to remarry by the laws of any
State and the rules of other Christian
churches.
WILL TEACH AT STANFORD.
Herbert Z. Kipp for Assistant In
structor in German.
STANFORD UNIVERSITY. June 21.
â The announcement was made to-day
of the appointment by President David
Starr Jordan of Herbert Z. Kipp to be
assistant professor of German. Mr.
Kipp is now studying in Germany, but
will come to Stanford in September to
assume his work. During the coming
year he will take the place of Professor
James O. Griffin, who Is now on his
way to Europe for a vacation and to
study, and will teach the course in first
and second year German.
Mr. Kipp was born in 1874, and
studied at Adelbert College, Western
Reserve, and at Princeton University,
where he graduated with honors in 1894.
He then traveled in Europe for a time,
and has been pursuing advanced
studies at the famous German univer
sities of Berlin, Gottingen and Leipzig.
He will take the Ph.D. degree at Leip
zig this summer.

TRUSTS ARE UNLAWFUL
UNDER IOWA'S LAWS
Attorney General Rsmley's Interest-
ing Address at a Convention
of County Attorneys.
DES MOINES, la., June 21.âAttor
ney General Remley, in an address to
day to the county attorneys of the
State, in convention here, declared that
trusts are unlawful under the lowa
laws. Ho advised county attorneys to
use their efforts to suppress trusts in
the State. Among other things the At
torney General said:
"There is no question that combina
tions entered into by large aggregations
of capital to increase prices or decrease
production of manufactures and mer
chandise of any kind which administer
to the necessities or comforts of men
are an evil, and nothing but an evil.
There is one criminal law to which I
wish to call your attention. It has been
in force for a number of years, and I
believe there has never been a crimi
nal prosecution under it. Section 5067
provides, among other things. 'It shali
be the duty of the Grand Jury to in
quire into and ascertain if there exists
any pool, trust or combination within
their respective counties.' The same
section says: "County attorneys, in
their counties, and the Attorney Gen
eral, shall enforce the provisions of a
public nature in the seven preceding
sections.' County attorneys and grimd
juries must take the initiative in this
matter. As legal advisers of the Grand
Jury you can do much to bring to jus
tice any violators of the anti-trust laws.
You can do your State no better service
than by either demonstrating the non
existence of unlawful pr-ols and trusts
in the State or by bringing the guilty
parties to justice. I pledge you here
and now that your efforts will be sec
onded by the Attorney General's office
and any assistance which I can give
will he most heartily given."

shoehorn.

the crowd that remained in the court
room in the early hours of the evening,
wishing to spare the accused woman
as much as possible. Finally the Coun
ty Clerk read the verdict:
"We, the jury, find the defendant
guilty."
The defendant was given until 10
o'clock to appear for sentence or make
a motion fur a new trial.
Mrs. Storke was indicted by the
Grand Jury in December last on a
charge of sending libelous letters to
Dr. S. A. Winchester. The twon had
been flooded with the anonymous let
ters making charges of the vilest na
ture against Dr. Winchester and Dr.
Martha <'ase, a young woman practic
ing here. Scores of letters were re
ceived in May. June and July, IS9B, and
the postal authorities took the matter
up. A detective traced the authorship
of the letters to Mrs. Storke, but. fear
ing the case could not be made in the
Federal Court, the matter was laid be
fore the Grand Jury.
Th" defense tried, to connect a num
ber of prominent persons here, includ
ing the divorced husband of Mrs.
Storke, with the authorship of the let
ters, claiming there was a conspiracy to
drive her from the city by connecting
her name with the letters. The trial
has occupied the Superior Court, Judge
( Â»ster of San Bernardino presiding, for
three arid a half weeks. Great interest
was taken in the trial, the court room
being crowded at all times.
Y<la Addis Storke was once well
known in Southern California news
paper circles and was considered a
brilliant writer.
Since tho cnmmoneoment of the libel
suit the wife of Dr. Winchester, th>
complaining witness, has instituted
proceedings for a divorce.

AT SANTIAGO
Fourteen New Cases and
Four Deaths.
_
AMERICAN TROOPS SUFFER
COMPELLED TO EVACUATE
THEIR BARRACKS.
Owing to the Improved Sanitary
Condition of the Cities in Cuba
It Had Been Hoped an Out
break Would Be Prevented.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
CALL HEADQUARTERS, WEL
LINGTON HOTEL, WASHINGTON.
June 21.â Surgeon General Sternberg is
â¢ absent from Washington, but I leaxn
j that he received communications from
i South America showing that the Sana
! relli serum was not producing as favor
| able results in yellow fever cases as its
: discoverer has announced.
Surgeon General Wyman of the Ma
rine Hospital service to-day received a
telegram from Dr. Park, the quarantine
; officer at Santiago, reporting the exist fe
| ence at that port of fourteen cases oi?
I yellow fever and four deaths from that
' cause. This is not the first report the
Marine Hospital service has received,
of the existence of the scourge at San-
I tiago.
Five days ago five cases were re
ported. There has not been time for a
' mail report since the outbreak. In ad-
I dition to the number of cases and
deaths Dr. Park's cablegram to-day
conveyed the information that the
American troops had evacuated the
barracks and their bedding and bag
gage were being disinfected aboard the
hospital boat Rough Rider, which is fit
ted with a complete disinfecting plant.
The fact that the season has advanced
thus far with the appearance only of
a few sporadic cases of the yellow fever
; led to the general hope that the im
proved sanitary condition of Santiago,
: Havana and other Cuban cities since
i the American occupation might result
in preventing an outbreak.
The following bulletin was posted at
the War Department:
"HAVANA, June 20.â Adjutant Gen
\ eral, Washington: Death report, 19th,
Santiago: Private David Mulhern,
, Company C, Fifth Infantry, died 18th,
I yellow fever; Private Thomas Jennings.
, band, Fifth Infantry, died 19th, yellow
fever. Puerto Principe â Private Elliot
; D. Holderman, Company D, Fifth In
fantry, died 19th, yellow fever; Private
; Louis J. Wagner, Company A, Fifteenth
Infantry, died 17th, accidental poison
ing. BROOKE, Commanding."

LOUISVILLE, Ky.. June 21.â The first
law of heaven had little to do with the
regulation of to-day's proceedings of
the Democratic State convention. On
the contrary order gave place to great
confusion, and after sweltering through
nearly eight hours of tedious hearing
of contests the convention, without
nominating a chairman, adjourned un
til evening in a whirlwind of chairs,
fans, tally sheets and reporters' copy.
Fortunately no one was hurt.
This finale was as unexpected as it
was lively. The sweltering, packed-in,
jammed-together mass of delegates,
politicians and onloc-kers had yelled it
self hoarse, fanned itself pallid. It was
more in a humor to do Justice to a din
ner than a prize-fight.
Feeling was at high tension over an j
argument that was in progress before j
Chairman Johnston over the contested j
ration from Kenton County, the i
home of William Goebel, one of the j
leading candidates for Governor. Con
gressman Ellis of the Sixth District, a
venerable gentleman of much vehe- i
inence. was eloquently presenting the
Ooebel side of the case, and was being
interrupted at short intervals by noisy
I demonstrations of approval. As much
<>f this interference came from the
stage, which was crowded with candi- I
dates, guests, officers of the convention \
and newspaper men, Chairman John- I
ston announced that the platform would
bo cleared at the next interruption. A
-00-pound ruralite, a little the worse for '
his political and spirituous excitement, |
mounted a chair and challenged Major
Johnston to carry out his threat, an
nouncing incidentally that force would
be necessary in his case. Quick"" as a
flash Jim Williams started from the
front of the stage for the intruder in a
businesslike way. Williams is a jovial
character of some repute in gunnery, j
and his activity was the signal for a '
stampede toward the exits. Almost â¢
everybody was standing on a chair j
when the affair occurred, and the re
suit was a general scramble to get out !
of the way of a general disarrange
ment of furniture. Half a dozen men
surrounded each of the belligerents and
the disturber was hustled out. Not i
however, until the crowd had cleared a |
space in the middle of the stage about !
thirty feet in diameter, having a fringe |
of huddled humanity around the edge, !
crouched in corners or behind shelter- I
ing walls. As no weapon was drawn I
this struck the humor of the crowd and !
quiet was restored, but meanwhile
Chairman Johnston had declared the
convention adjourned until evening
Aside from this incident the day's
session was a dull monotony, varied ;
only by an occasional heated discussion
between heads of rival delegations or
an outburst of applause as one or an
other condidate scored an advantage in '
the roll call for: temporary chairman i
For this position the Stone-Goebel I
combination nominated Judge David B. I
Red wine of Breathett County, while
the Hardin men named Hon. W. H
Sweeney of Marion County. When the
roll had been called once Redwine had
r.4o'Â£ of the votes and Sweeney 428 1 / Â£,
but 150 votes had been passed; on ac
count of contests. These were taken
up by counties, and Chairman Johnston
of the State Central Committee heard
arguments by both sides before recog
nizing a contested delegation. This
consumed the entire day's session and
caused changes which left the vote at
adjournment as follows: Redwine,
569%; Sweeney, 528^.
When the convention got together
this evening Chairman Johnston took
the Kenton County contest under ad
visement, after which he decided in
favor of the regular delegates in two
districts and threw out the third.
Johnston then announced the result of
the ballot for temporary chairman, de
claring Redwine elected by a vote of I
551 5-6 to 529 1-6 for Sweeney. This I
marked the success of the first step In
the Stone-Ooebel combination. The an
nouncement was received with tumult
uous applause. Judge Redwine made a
brief speech of thanks to the conven- i
tion and assumed the chair. After em
powering the chairman to appoint com
mittee members of the State at large
the convention adjourned until to
morrow morning.

In the river just as the r-hannel is ap

MILES NOT SENT
TO PHILIPPINES
PRESIDENT
SATISFIED
WITH OTIS
Insurgent Army Takes to
the Hills and Swamps
Beyond Imus.
Sppoial Dispatch to The Call.
CALL HE ADQUARTERS,
WELLING TON HOTEL.
WASHINGTON, June 21.â
It is authoritatively denied that the
President has any intention of send
ing Major General Miles to the Phil
ippines. Major General Otis is con
ducting the campaign satisfactorily,
and the President does not propose
to reflect upon him by dispatching
his senior to relieve him. General
Miles told me to-day tnat he had not
applied for service in Manila, and
he had heard nothing from the Presi
dent to lead him to suppose that that
official proposed to give him orders to
proceed to that point.
MANILA, June 21, 3:21 p. in.â The in
surgent army has taken to the swamps
and hills beyond Imus. The largest
force is supposed to he in the neigh
borhood of San Francisco de la Mala
bon, holding a posrtion more toward
the lake or toward the coast, acnrd
ing as exigencies demand.
General "Wheaton returned to Imus
to-day, bringing three men who were
wounded in yesterday's fighting.
The heavy rains that fall nightly
make campaigning uncomfortable. The
roads are still good, but the rice fields
adjoining them are pools of water.
The Filipinos will not fight unless
they can meet our troops in positions
most advantageous to themselves or
take our reeÂ«>nnoitering bodies in am
bush.
It is impossible to learn what effect
the recent defeat has had upon their
leaders. This should be disheartening,
for they had boasted that the Amer
icans could never conquer the province
of Cavlte, Aguinaldo's home county,
where he always worsted the Span
iards.
General Otis recently received a let
ter signed by native women of the
province declaring that if all the men
were killed the women would still keep
up the fight against the Americans.
Copies of the insurgent organ, Inde
pendencia. show that the Filipinos'
hopes nf success are kept aflame by po
litical movements in America.
The Independencia prints reports of
alleged speeches made at alleged meet
ings in the United States denouncing
the war, and it a-sserts that these repre
sent the dominant American sentiment.
It declares also that the FllipinÂ«rs will
"ntinue the war until the next Presi
dential campaign, which is "sure to re
sult in a decision to withdraw the
American troops from the Philippines."
It is reported that the Spanish garri
son at Baler, in the province of Prin
cipe, on the east coast of Luzon, which
had become reduced to thirty-three
m^n, finally surrendered to the Fili
pinos after holding out for a year. The
Filipinos imprisoned two Englishmen at
Tachbobona. on the Island of Samar.
southeast of Luzon. The British first
class cruiser Grafton steamed to the
point from Cebu and their commander
demanded their release. When this was
refused he landed a force of marines
and cleared his ship for action, where
upon the Englishmen were handed over.

plementary credits for the protectorates

defended the acquisition of the Spanish

i had long entertained, as iuwould tend to

sessions. He added that he was no

In the case of Kiaorhau, and asserted he
', win justified In assuming that the new

commercially, for German industry and
(Jerman enterprise in those regions

vance under much more favorable con
ditions. The islands, he explained, were
admirably suited for cultivation and

THE SAIS FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, JUNE 22, 1899.
SENATOR BATE NOT
FOR IMPERIALISM
SENATOR W. B. BATE OF TENNESSEE.
FRESNO, June 21.â United States Senator W. B. Bate of Tennessee, who
has been visiting relatives in this city for the past week or so, left
last night for Los Angeles to visit his daughter, Mrs. Childs, and his
Bister, Mrs. Tyree of that city. In an interview before leaving Senator
Bate expressed his views on public topics quite plainly.
"I opposed Hawaiian annexation," said the Senator, "because it was in
violation of the principles of the Monroe doctrine, and because it represents
a violation of the spirit and genius of our Government; moreover, because
we want no such representatives as the races of Hawaii and the Philippine
Islands would send us. I am equally oppcrsed to the colonial system."
Senator Bate said this system would cause us to follow in the footsteps of
monarchies. It would lead us in the path that led republican Rome to de
struction. Colonization means centralization; centralization means imper
ialism and a large standing army. This last is one of the evil consequences
to be dreaded from the present war in the Philippines.
"The fact that I was elected without opposition," continued the Senator,
"indicates that the people of Tennessee are in harmony with this view.
The people of the South are opposed to expansion. On this coast, and espe
cially in San Francisco. I have found the expansion principle largely fa
vored and advocated by the wealthy classes and wholesale merchants, and
by those who are commonly known as goldbugs."
Bate said a very small part of the Populists will refrain from co-operat
ing with the Democracy, especially if Mr. Bryan Is the leader. Another
thing which is encouraging is that a large portion of the German element In
the Northwestern States, which are doubtful, is in sympathy with the De
mocracy and against expansion.
America and Germany. From the out-
Bet the Minister also said, Germany
could dispense with the costly military
establishments of Spain. During the
course of his remarks Baron yon Rulow
said: ''We hold it to be our first duty to
see that our relations with other powers
are undisturbed by the new acquisition,
and hope that our relations with the im
mediate neighbors of the islands, name
ly, the Americans and Japanese, will only
become more intimate. We have not
thought of placing ourselves in opposi
tion to the Americans in the South Pa
cific, and we as little wish to prejudice
American interest? as America contem
plates injuring us without cause or rea
son." , ,
The Reichstag: subsequently passed the
second reading of the credit for the pur
chase of the islands, with the loan bill
and the commercial convention with
Spain attached.

ing information concerning the fate of
the Baron. Adjutant General Corbln
telegraphed to Major General Otis, who
replied as follows: "A committee of ;
citizens sent Citizen dv Marais to the j
insurgent authorities to intercede for j
the release of the Spanish prisoners.
The last information regarding him was '
that he was with Luna's forces at Cal
umpit, before the town was captured.
It is the belief of many citizens here
that Dv Marais was murdered. Others j
believe that he is held a prisoner."
INGERSOLL TALKS
AS AN EXPANSIONIST
PHILADELPHIA, June 21.â Colonel
Robert G. Ingersoll, in response to a re
quest for his views on the Philippine
question, said to-night:
"I am an expansionist. This country
has land hunger and expansion is popu
lar. J want all we can honestly set. But
I do not want the Philippines unless the
Filipinos want us. and I feel exactly the
same about the Cubans. We must act
nobly to the Filipinos, whether we got
the "islands or not. I would like to bee
peace between us and the Filipinosâ peace
honorable to both; peace based on reason
Instead of force."

Mary Wanted to Die.
Mary Casnero, a Mexican girl, nineteen
years of age, who lives on Hinckley alley,
attempted suicide last night by swallow
ing a dose of precipitate of mercury. She
was taken to the Harbor Receiving Hos
pital, where she was treated by Dr. yon
der Lelth. She recovered sufficiently In
a short time to be able to go home. She
says that she had no money and preferred
suicide to death by starvation.
Fireman Jumps Overboard.
Harry Lane, a man about 45 years .of
age, who shipped on the tug Rescue as a j
fireman on her recent trip to Coos Bay,
jumped overboard on the trip. His body
was recovered by the sailors and brought
to this city, arriving last night, and was
taken to ihe Morgue.
DENTISTS ACCOMPLISH
GREAT DEAL OF WORK
INTERESTING DEMONSTRATIONS
MADE IN CLINICS.
At the Night Session Several In
structive Papers Were Read and
Discussed at Length.
Much work was accomplished yesterday
at the second day's session of the twenty
eighth annual convention of the California
Dental Association. The morning' session
was devoted to routine business and the
appointment of several important com
mittees, among which was a committee
to examine into the matter of having
dentists appointed to the army and navy.
The matter of appointing delegates to the
national convention of the association was
left to the president. The following appli
cants for membership in the association,
having been approved by the board of
trustees, were elected: "W. R. Linscott,
A. J. Hineker. G. W. Earl, G. H. Kreich
baum, J. Moran Pearce. W. A. Atwood
C R. "Wilcoxon. T. A. Vogel and C. S.
Zelgler. A paper on "Dental Education"
w;ts read by I'r. W. A. Knowles, and one
on "The Choice of Proper Filling Ma
terials" by Dr. J. Foster Flagg. Both
papers were discussed at some length by
the dentists.
The afternoon session was devoted en
tirely to the clinics and proved to be ex
tremely interesting. Dr. S. L. Strickland
of San Francisco demonstrated cataphor-
Fsis and rool canal filling: Dr. G. E. Stall
man of San Francisco demonstrated metal
posts for abutments in crown and bridge
work; Dr. A. M. Barker of San Jose dem
onstrated insertion of disto-ocolusal gold
lining without matrix, using Perry sepa
rator: T)r. A. F. Merrlam of wakland
demonstrated the use of the napkin In the
mouth in gold filling; Dr. E. R. Tait of
' Oakland showed the use of creosoted
charcoal points in alveolar abscess "with
treatment; Dr. A. C. Hart of San Fran
cisco showed treatment for the prevention
of decay in the teeth and ajso the making
of an. antiseptic mouth wash; Dr. Rus
sell Hopkins of San Francisco showed the
process of implantation and the use of
silk ligatures in orthodontla, and Dr C
Deiehmiller of San Francisco gave a dem
onstration of combination gold and ce
ment filling and the administration of
chloroform by means of an inhaler admit
ting atmospheric air.
The night session was prefaced by a
general discussion of the afternoon work
in clinics. This proved to be very inter
esting and gratifying to the members of
the association. The following and ex
haustive papers were then read and dis
cussed: "The Conduct of Practice." Dr
T>. Van Orden of San Francisco; "Some
of Our Failuresâ Their Cause and Rem
edy." Dr. W. J. Taylor of Sacramento
and "What Constitutes an Ethical Prac
titioner," Dr. Frank L. Platt of San
Francisco.
The third day of the convention prom
ises to see much work accomplished, and
the dentists will be called to order at 9\V)
o'clock this morning in the auditorium of
the Y. M. C. A. building.
ATTACHES THE SCENERY.
H. P. Ducket Wants Pay for Work
Done for Japanese Theatrical
Manager.
Deputy Sheriff Leon Veiller last night
at 8 o'clock served an attachment against
S. K. Mitsusp, manager of the Japanese
company now performing at the Califor
nia. The attachment was for $250, and
was in favor of H. P. Ducket, who
painted some special scenery for Mit
suse'a company. The matter was ad
.iustPd temporarily by S. H. Friedlander
manager of the California Theater who
assured the deputy that he would' hold
the scenery for Ducket. With this as
surance the scenery was allowed to be
used for the performance last night
Friedlander said last night that the
money was due Ducket, and that he did
not have the slightest doubt that he
would be paid.
HOLLS RECEIVES
NO SATISFACTION
So He Starts to Find the
Kaiser.
PEACE WORK AT THE HAGUE
GLOOMY VIEW OF EUROPEAN
PRESS PREMATURE.
Delegates at the Conference Yet Hope
to Accomplish Something in
the Way of Mediation
and Arbitration.
Special Cable to The Call and the New York
Herald. Copyrighted, 1899, by James Gor
don Bennett.
BERLIN. June 21.â Mr. Holls
has been to Berlin, and yester
day managed to see both the
Chancellor and Minister of For
eign Affairs. Not obtaining any
satisfaction, he started off to
find the Kaiser, who to-morrow
arrives at Kiel.
THE HAGUE, June 21.â The best in
formed circles here do not share the
gloomy views of the European press
regarding the peace conference. After
a week's adjournment, the arbitration
committee met for a short time to-day.
Dr. Zorn of the German delegation and
Frederick Holls of the American dele
gation attended. Mr. Holls expressed
himself as satisfied with the results of
the trip he took to Berlin in company
with Dr. Zorn, and the German dele
gates have been instructed to take part
in the deliberations of the arbitration
committee which will now continue
brisk work pending the final decision of
Germany.
The disarmament committee, to
whom was submitted the circular of
Count Muravieff, has considered the
second, third and fourth paragraphs
in .sub-committee, the result being a
negative vote upon all three, on the
ground that it would be impossible to
decide upon a satisfactory plan for
checking the continued improvements
in explosive ordnance. This decision
wlli be submitted to the committee to
morrow. The first paragraph of the
circular, which proposes to arrest the
increase of land and sea forces and war
budgets, has not yet been touched.
The Russians, its is said, intend to in
troduce fresh proposals, and it is not
doubted that the conference will reach
important proposals on the last four
paragraphs of the circular supplement
ing the Geneva convention and the
Brussels conference, and providing for
mediation and arbitration.

NEW AGRICULTURAL DISTRICT
Preliminary Steps Taken at Wood
land for Its Formation.
WOODLAND. June 21.â At a public
mooting- held on Tuesday evening- the pre
liminary steps were taken toward the
formation of a new agricultural district,
for which the last Legislature made an
appropriation. The following gentlemen
were named as eligible for directors:
Charles Hoppin of Yolo. John Reith Jr.
of Davisville, C. A. Stevens of Cadenassa
J. O. Chalmers, C. F. Burns. A. M. Britt!
Charles Nelson and Sam Montgomery of
Woodland.
The secretary was instructed to notify
these gentlemen to meet at the law office's
of A. W. North, in this city, next Satur
day afternoon at 2 o'clock for the purpose
of organization. At that time the officers
must be 'elected and the articles of incor
poration prepared in legal form. The
Governor will then be notified of the ac
tion of the meeting of the association and
the election of Its directors, the meeting
of the directors and the Incorporation
It Is then the duty of the Governor to ap
point eight directors, but he is not re
stricted to the names selected by the
meeting 1 .

DRUIDS HONOR
SIEG'S MEMORY
.Services Held at His
Grave.
BEAUTIFUL FLORAL TRIBUTES
LODGE OFFICERS PRONOUNCE
EULOGIES.
Grand Grove Will To-Day Name the
Men Who Shall Conduct Its
Affairs for the Ensuing
Term.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
PLACERVILLE, Cal., June 21.â The
Grand Grove of the United Ancient
Order of Druids devoted its forenoon
session to business. Two brothers re
ceived the grand arch degree. A reso
lution making an appropriation for the
support of the Druids' Magazine was
adopted. ..e decisions of the noble
grand arch in the appeals of Mazzini
Grove and B. Pellegini, involving pro
cedure in the trials of brothers before
the subordinate groves, were upheld by
the Grand C .ye upon the recommen
dation of the committee on appeals and
grievances.
The proposition to change the right
of representation from one represen
tative for every twenty-five members
to one representative for every fifty
members provoked the most important
discussion of the session. Numerous
representatives took part, among whom
were Past Noble Grand Arch Goller, in
favor of the proposition, and Past No
ble Grand Arch Devoto against the
change. The proposition was almost
unanimously voted down.
The election of grand officers will
take place to-morrow. The contest for
deputy grand arch is assuming inter
esting proportions. Six candidates are
in the field and it is difficult to predict
who will be the successful one. Hagen,
Lange, Godeau, Schord and O'Donneil
are the leading candidates.
At the afternoon session of the Grand
Grove reports of committees were re
ceived and adopted.
At 4 o'clock p. m. the Grand Grove
adjourned and in a body proceeded to
Union Cemetery, where it held me
morial services at the grave of the late
Frederick Sieg, P. G. N. A., the founder
of Druidism on the Pacific Coast. The
grave was decorated with many beauti
ful floral pieces and wreaths of oak and
mistletoe, and eloquent addresses on
the life and character of Mr. Sieg were
delivered by Past Noble Grand Arch
Devoto and District Deputy Grand
Arch Mayer of this city.
To-night the visitors were entertained
with an open-air concert, a ball at the
opera-house and a banquet at Confi
dence Hall.
FET KEE'S FACTORY
SEIZED FOR FRAUD
CHINESE PUNKS SOLD FOR IM-
PORTED WEEDS.
Internal Revenue Department De
frauded by the Use of Refilled
Cigar Boxes and Uncan
celed Stamps.
Burt M. Thomas, special agent of in
ternal revenue, conferred a favor upon
cig-ar smokers yesterday by seizing the
cigar factory of the Capitol Cigar Com- j
pany at 649 Clay street and placing its
proprietor, Fet Kee. alias Choo Yee. un
der arrest for refilling cigar boxes with
cigars and for placing cigars in old boxes
on which the revenue stamps had not |
been destroyed and for using an imitation
imported label.
Several weeks ago Agent Thomas dis
covered that Chinese-made cigars were
being sold under popular brands of Key
West and Havana firms. These inferior
cigars were sold at $40 per thousand, while
the genuine article which they were re- j
placing were selling at from $S0 to $90 per
thousand.
Mr. Thomas traced the counterfeit j
weeds to a white retail dealer, whose i
name he declines to make public. This
dealer had been in the habit of bringing
the empty cigar boxes to the factory and
having them tilled with the Chinese-made
article. By leaving the old revenue
stamps on the boxes he was enabled to
save the tax, which amounts to $3 <>0 per
thousand. Fet Yee. being in the business
of making and selling cigars, filled the
boxes without question and took his
money with thanks.
The white man was given four of the
empty boxes found in his possession after
they had been privately marked with the
initials of the revenue officials and were !
sent to the factory. Fet Kee filled them
with 4-cent cigars, but the white man had
but money sufficient to pay for two boxes,
which he took away, leaving the other
two in the factory to be called for. They
were called for about an hour afterward
by Mr. Thomas and posse and were found
In the factory neatly filled.
The factory, containing 200.000 cigars, !
besides h large quantity of tobacco, was
seized. The value of the plant and stock
is about $10,000. The penalty for Fet Kee's
offense is the confiscation of the* entire
plant and stock and imprisonment for six
months to two years, with a fine added.
Such cases are usually compromised on
payment of a money penalty.
MRS. PARRY ARRAIGNED.
She Secures Her Release on New
Bonds Which Are Considered
Satisfactory.
Mrs. May Parry was arraigned in Judgo
Graham's court yesterday morning on the
charge of manslaughter )n causing the |
death of her husband. She was heavily
veiled and was accompanied by her
mother, Mrs. Van Valkenburg, and her
attorney. George A. Knight. The court
room was crowded.
She was instructed as to her rights by
the Judge and arraigned, and by consent
the preliminary examination was con
tinued till June 2S. This concluded the
proceedings.
Attorney Knight secured the release of
the defendant on a new bond, the sure
ties being Attorney Knight and the de
fendant's mother, who gave further de
tails as to her property in Woodland on
which she qualified to Bond Expert New
man.

AND VENEZUELA

dary Commission.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
PARIS, June 21. â The Venezuelan bound
ary commission reassembled at 11 o'clock
this morning, and Sir Richard Webster, I
the British Attorney General, resumed his
speech In behalf of the case of Great Brit
ain. Dealing with the period from WOO to
1637, he said Great Britain was unable to 1
rely on the Spanish records of Venezuelan
control and was compelled to fall back
upon the discovery and circumnavigation,
so called, of the unit of Guiana. Venezuela
arguing that Guiana was a defined area,
that Spain had discovered that defined
area and had taken part, In the name of
the svholi -taking such possession as to J
tntitle them to the whole.
In close detail Sir Richard Webster ex
amined th< work of the various explorers
of the period upon whom Venezuela relied,
claiming that they were not relevant to
the territory of Guiana, adding that the I
statements made before the United States
commission must not be argued against
Great Britain, as frequent deductions were
made which were totally unsupported, as
Justice Brewer, a former memoer of .hut
commission, would doubtless member.
According 10 counsel for Great Britain the
first explorer who really reached viuiana I

the Orinoco before 1720."
As to the Spanish coasting voyages. Sir '
Richard Webster said the Spaniards occa- |
sionally traded, but the Dutch traded and <
Settled. By the truce of 1009 the Dutch j
Bltion was known and acknowledged by :
pain, and, as Motley, the hostorian, had

cate with the sword that sovereignty
wrlrh Spanish words and genius bad
once acquired."
Continuing, the British counsel claimed
that discover}', when not followed by oc
cupation, v.as of no value as the basis of
title; Inasmuch as nations had never con
rented to the contrary doctrine. Discov
ery, if accompanied by a certified or noto
rious Intention to acquire, gave a prior op
portunity of acquiring, which opportunity
it would be a violation of the comity of
nations to Interfere with prematurely.
The occupation of vacant Darts of the
world treated as not in the possession cf
a distant owner was a case of transfer of

push. Guiana was vacant. The Dutcn tet

Secretary of the Interior Dismisses an
Interesting California Land
Contest.
WASHINGTON. June 21.â The Secretary
of the Interior has dismissed the contest
in the case of Lauchlin â¢ Robertson vs.
Peter O'Hagan for homestead entry on a
tract of land in the Visalia land district.
The contest was based on abandonment
and the locaJ Land Office decided in fa
vor of Robertson. O'Hagan then appeal
ed to General Land Commissioner Her

MUNICIPAL OWNERSHIP
OF ITS WATER SYSTEM
REAL ESTATE OWNERS CONFER
WITH MAYOR PHELAN.
Fully $800,000 Would Be Saved the
City Annually by Constructing
Its Own Plant.
A number of members of the Real Es
tate Owners' Association held a confer
ence with Mayor Phelan yesterday to dis
cuss municipal ownership of water works.
They were in session for some time and i
the matter was thoroughly gone over.
The Mayor called attention to chapter i
121 of the new charter, which bears di
rectly upon the matter by providing that
within one year after the charter lea
Into effect and every two years thereafter !
until the object is accomplished the City |
Engineer must submit plans and esti
mates for a system of water supply. \
There were two propositions under dis- ;
cussionâthe acquisition of the existing |
water supply and original construction.
Mr. Harris, one of the members of the I
association, was the principal speaker,
and he strongly opposed the acquisition
of the existing work, which he termed a
patchwork system, with no stable supply
of pure water.
Mayor Pbelan stated that if the city
spent $20,000,000 for a water system it i
couM sell its bonds at :;'; per cent, which I
would amount to $700,000 annually for in
terest. Based on the Spring Valley Com- \
pany's own figures on what it cost to op
erate thai plant last year, which are ex
cessive, the cost of maintenance would be .
$4*60.000 making a total expenditure of
51.1C0.000 per annum. It now costs the
city for water 51.9W.000 a year, which
would mean a clear saving of $800,000 to
the city. The Mayor said that $20,000,000 !
would bring water from Lake Tano< or I
the North Fork of the American River j
and distribute it. Engineer Smith esti
mate! the cost at $13,000,000, while Colonel
Mendcll had said it would *ost nearer
i17.000.ii00. so the Mayor had allowed an
even $20,000,000 for the work in his figures. I
The conference adjourned after the mem
bers â  ad promised to agitate the subject
among their friends throughout the city.

RUHLIN IS TO
TAKE THE LION'S
SHARE OF PURSE
Follows Fashion Set
by Fitz.
HE IS THE STAR BOXER;
-â  â¦
KENNEDY HAS TO MAKE CON
CESSIONS."
â¦
i "Will Try to Equal the Brilliant Ex- j
ample of Jeffries De Witt
Van Court Chosen
Referee.
Win or lose on Friday night, Gus Ruhlin
will take two-thirds of the purse offered
by the Excelsior Athletic Club for his
meeting with Joe Kennedy in the arena !
at Woodward's Pavilion. He is the star,
â  and following the fashion set by Fitssim
j mons, demanded the right .to dictate the
terms under which the bout will tako
place. He would not agree to make the
match otherwise, and Kennedy, ambitious I
; 10 meet a man of some reputation, was I
I compelled to accept these terms or look I
elsewhere for an opponent.
The fact that this arrangement exL-stJ
does not carry with it the Imputation that
; the men will not contest every bit of Ihe
way. On the contrary, it has urged the
California!! on to greater efforts and he '
will do all he can to be returned the â¢
victor. It means other matches, where ho
, may perhaps have the right to dictate his
terms. The same agreement was made
in the championship battle at Coney Isl
: and recently, but the spectators saw al>
the sport they desired in those eleven
! rounds of desperate lighting. Ruhlin still

The Watsonville team has passed from
the management of I'M Struvc and is now
controlled by a committee of citizens,
which has installed Harper as field man
ager, with full control of the players.
Some new talent is lining negotiated" for,
as it is the committee's intention to make
the team the strongest in the league, both
in fielding and batting. Harper's promo
tion is most deserving, for he thoroughly
understands the game and is an excellent :
tactician.
It might he a good Idea for Manager
Ewing to try Russell in two games
against the Lobsters this week. Their
heaviest batters are left-handers and have
little success against south side twlrlers.
The local team hits hard enough to win
almost any B'amc, but stupid base run
ning costs it many runs. A few judicious
fines by Manager Harris might teach his
men how to act on the plates.
San Jose is now playing an almost per
fect fielding game, but its batting- is lam
entably weak. Chief Borchers ought to
make his men practice batting every day,
sending in his fast delivery himself for
them to work on. With their batting
lamps properly trimmed the Gardeners
will be in it.

ALMOST A MILE
A MINUTE MADE
BY A CYCLIST
Locomotive Sets the
Pace.
MARVELOUSLY FAST RIDE
DISTANCE COVERED IN SIXTY
FIVE SECONDS.
The Wheelman Shielded From the
Wind by a Contrivance Built
Upon His Steam-Driven
Pacemaker.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK, June 21.â A man on a
bicycle rode a mile to-day faster than
any one ever before traveled the same
distance by self-propulsion. The man
was Charles M. Murphy, and he rode
the mile in sixty-five seconds. It was
done as honestly, cleanly and in as
sportsmanlike a manner as any bicycle
ever was ridden. His sole advantage
was a locomotive with one car at
tached, which set the pace and enabled
him to ride in a partial vacuum. No
straps or tow ropes or harness were
employed. He rode naturally, simply
keeping close to the car by rapid pedal
ing. It was a wonderful performance
and a spectacle of thrilling interest. .
The scene of the ride was the central
branch of the Long Island Kailroad,
five miles long, between Farmingdale
and Babylon.
At the rear of the car a box-like
structure had been built to shield the
; rider from the wind. The sides ex
! tended straight out six feet and down
ward to within two feet of the ground,
where they slanted inward toward the
track at an angle of 40 degrees. The
bottoms of the wind-shielding exten
sion lapped over the inner sides of the
rails, setting close to them like flanges
to exclude all the air and dust possible.
\\ iihin the shielding Fides of this con
trivance Murphy pedaled for more than
i two miles, swerving only for a few
' inches and never losing his tremendous,
I steam-driven pacemaker. lie was bare
i headed and wore no eyeglasses, nose or
mouth shield or any kind of appliance.
He rode a bicycle of the regular stock
racing model, weighing twenty-one
pounds and geared to 112, with ordinary
wire toeclips.
There were three-quarters of a mile
to- be traveled before the real trial be
gan, for the board track was more than
two miles long, and nearly a half-mile
was left for slowing up. The cyclist
pedaled faster and faster, his wheel
never failing away an inch from its
Position and swaying from side to side
only five inches. There were five
planks, each ten inches wide, compos
ing the track. Murphy rode the entl it
distance on the middle c-ne and used
only half of that.
As the starting lino was crossed there
was a slight but perceptible increase In
the speed <>f the engine. "Surely the
rider must be lost," thought tin- watch
| ers; but Murphy, except for a slight
I side swaying, did not change his posi
tion. From start to finish the rate of
I speed varied only by the fractions of
a second in each quarter-mile.
Spectators had previously ridden over
the course in one minute and eight
seconds when the engineer was trying
himself and his engine Then it seemed
lik" a flying leap from one end of the
board to the other, but now with a
cyclist riding there under their feet it
seemed like an hour.
The first quarter was traveled in
:16 2-5; the second in :16 3-5, and the
third in :16 1-6.
As the lust quarter was begun the
car bounded just a little from some
unknown cause. The cyclist then fell
away for the <>nly time in the ride.
He dropped back four or five inches and
every heart stood still, but a second
more and he was back again, his legs
moving like piston rods as before.
When the finish line was crossed the
rider threw up his head and Hashed
a grinning countenance at the watch
ers.
The engineer, as soon as the finishing j
mark was crossed, opened the throttle j
and the train bounded away at seventy
miles an hour, thereby leaving Murphy,
who probably would have been unable
to slow away from the car had this not
been done.
Murphy is convinced he can ride a
mile a minute, and so is every one who
witnessed th< j trial to-day. He will
make the attempt on June 30. at the
State League of American Wheelmen
meet.

CLASH OVER DATES.
Oakland and Stockton Races to Occur
at the Same Time.
STOCKTON. June 21.â There may be a
clash between Oakland and Stockton over
the dates for the fall races. The local
Driving Club, which le giving the fair
and race meet this year, has decided to
hold the latter during the week beginning
September 18. The club claims the right
to the old Stockton dates, but it is un
derstood that Oakland is accepting en
tries for that time. If the Driving Club
finds all the good harness events are go
ing to Oakland, it is proposed to make

suffering from hemorrhage of the brain
and that he was completely paralyzed.
It is doubtful if he lives till daylight.
Peppers left the theater and the police
are now looking for him. Houseman and
Siler have notified the authorities where
they may be found if wanted.
GODDARD LOSES ON A FOUL.
Strikes His Colored Opponent When.
the Latter Is Down.
CHICAGO. June 21.â Joe Goddard, the
Australian heavy-weight, lost on a foul
to "Klondike." a local colored heavy
weight, in the fifth round of what was to
have been a six round contest at the
Adelphi Theater to-night. After the first
round the men apparently forgot all about
science and went at it rough and tumble.
In the fourth round Goddard threw the
colored man and hit him while on the
floor.

Enterprises of this nd have become common to
the British by reason of the fact that they have long
had a surplus capital seeking investment in all parts
of the world. It is only recently, however, that the
capitalists of the United States have given them even
a casual consideration. Our own country has afforded
such inviting opportunities of ail kinds that we have
imported capital from abroad and have not thought of
ending it to other land; A change has come over
the minds of Eastern capitalists of late. They are no
longer content with the inducements offered by the
West and the South for investments, but are rivaling
the British themselves in the extent to which they are
venturing enterprises beyond the seas.
The Philadelphia Inquirer notes the undertaking of
an enterprise, under cor.trol of men in that city, to
establish woolen mills in China. Cotton and silk
have long been successfully manufactured by the
cheap labor of the Orient, and the promoters of the
row enterprise reasonably expect to accomplish
| equally good results in the product of woolen goods.

MAYOR P. S. RECTOR OF NEVADA CITY.
MAYOR B. B. RECTOR ol Nevada City la as stanch a sup; rtei

NEVADA CITY. June 21.â T0 the Editor of the San Fran
cisco Call: The proposition to erect a monument to the gallant
hero of Manila Bay is one that should receive net only the in
dorsement but tb* practical aid of every Californian who loves
his country and admires its defenders. If each of us contrib
utes a small amount there will be no hardship Imposed on any
body and the aggregate sum realized will be ample to build
the magnificent monument that is suggested. Nevada City can
be depended upon to respond with the same promptness and
generosity that she always does when appealed to in behalf of
a worthy cause. I will shortly name a committee of citizens to
take charge of the work here. B. S. RECTOR. Mayor.

CONSTERNATION
IN THE COUNTY
CLERK'S OFFICE
The Force Must Be
Greatiy Reduced.
The Justices of the Supreme Court yes
terday handed down a decision which
caused consternation in the County
Clerk's office and cast a deep gloom into
the hearts of nearly a hundred employes,
many of whom stand every chance of
losing their political jobs.
The decision was in the case of the
city and county of San Francisco against
William Broderlck, as Auditor. The ac
tion was instituted last year to enjoin the
Auditor from allowing- the demands of
certain assistant registry clerks and copy
ists appointed by the then County Clerk
Curry. The Auditor demurred to the pe
tition and his demurrer was sustained.
The petitioner refused to amend and
judgment of dismissal was ordered, from
which an appeal was taken. Th Su
preme Court says that the principal ques
tion is not as much the right of the Clerk
to appoint as it is of the duty of the city
to pay the salaries of his appointees. The
court says:
Section -â â  â¢'â¢-.â¢ county government act i; I
the section containing the authority -which it is j
contended authorized these appointments. It I
is a general section and read? as follows: j
"Every county, township and district officer, I
except a Supervisor or judicial officer, may mp- i
point as many deputies as may be firmans j
for th- prompt and faithful discharge of tna
duties of his office * â¢ â¢ ." This is 'â¢â¢.-
tainly a grant of power from the Legislature
to the â -.â ??â : en at officers, authorizing then to
appoint necessary deputies, bat the question 1
of the compensation of these deputies when i
appointed, is auite 3 different matter. It does
not fo!!cw that because the deputies may be |
appointed, their salaries must be paid by the >
county. * â¢ â¢ Such a construction would re- â¢
nit in a grievous interference with the coun
ty funds and would come clearly within the
purview of section 13. article XI of the con- '
stitution. â¢ â¢ *
In conclusion the court says:
The County Clerk of the city and county of
San Francisco by the act of February 13, 1880,
is given a fixed salary, and is allowed a fiiM
number of deputies and assistants, whose com
pensation la a charge upon the treasury. He ;
has the Dower under section 6! of the County
Government Act to anoint additional depu
tie? ar.l a c <;star.t<= If the needs of his office so I
require, but in the present condition of thÂ» j
law the compensation of these extra assist
ants and deputies must bÂ« paid for by himself
The Judgment Is therefore reversed, with di
rections to the trial court to overrule the Au
ditor's demurrer
In the case of Harry Piper, as chief {
deputy clerk with a salary of $250 per
month, the court says: "The office here
In question was created by an act of the
Legislature, approved February 14 lg?i
Judgment appealed from is affirmed
Under the decision the County Clerk
is allowed eight courtroom clerks, eight

;ee of eighteen; who in turn are to estab
lish headquarters and conduct the pri
mary campaign in the interest of the gen
?ral committee. It is asserted that in the
appointment of this committee of eigh
teen that the programmers violated the
trust reposed in the local committee by
!he Democratic State Central Committee.
The 100 were, appointed by the State
Central Committee, and simultaneous
with their appointment they were handed
a. constitution for their government.
Section 2 of said constitution reads:
"The Democratic City and County Com
mittee shall have no power to delegate to
my r>ther committee or persons any of the
powers hereby conferred on it except as
aerein provided."
Section 3 reads:
"The Democratic City and County Com
mittee, shall have power to appoint all dis
:rict or precinct committees and all sub
committees that it shall from time to time
item expedient, solely for the purpose of
Investigating and reporting to said Demo
:rat!c City and County Committee upon
any matter with regard to which It may
oe directed to investigate and report."
Section 4 reads:
"None of said committees shall con
:inue in existence except for the purpose
5f reporting to the Democratic City and
County Committee beyond the time of the
aext meeting of the Â»aid Democratic City
and County Committee and not longer
than thirty days In any event."
The committee of eighteen was not ap
pointed to Investigate anything. It was
appointed solely and simply for the pur
pose of conducting a campaign in the
Interest of the "bosses," who control the
committee of one hundred. These would
be bosses have discovered that it Is much
easier to manage a small committee of
eighteen than the larger one of 100, which
has already, so it is said, shown itself
unwieldy and difficult to handle. At the
meeting at which this executive com
mittee was provided for by resolution
there were only forty-five members pres
ent, and section 15 of the constitution
reads:
"No action of the Democratic City and
I County Committee or its successors shall
be valid or of any effect except as herein
otherwise provided, unless said action is
taken by a vote of three-fifths of all the
members of said Democratic City and
County Committee."
It will be observed that even if the
100 had the power to transfer any author
ity to an executive committee, then the
resolution creating the executive com
mittee did not have the necessary votes
to carry it. Sixty votes were necessary,
and as only forty-five were present it is
difficult to understand by what process
of reasoning Chairman McDonald de
clared the resolution carried. The Demo
cratic voters were absolutely ignored
when the Committee of One Hundred was
originally created: and now the said com
mittee has absolutely ignored the consti
tution which it was pledged to support.
It is noted that the several factions of
the Democratic party are now extremely
active in the enrollment of voters. The
keenest observers are not able to explain
fully the significance of this early activity.
Buckley's strength in the party is stiil
an unknown quantity. He is working
nitrht and day and constantly receiving
suggestions from lieutenants who were
at. one time rated as successful politi
cians. The problem is still unsolved
whether Buckley is in the field to organ
ize a machine for the future control of
k the party, or simply taking part in th's
I campaign to serve the cause of some big
corporation. Openly he is not oppose I to
the renomination of Mayor Phelan.
Whether his attitude in this respect will
change after the election of delegates to
the convention is a matter of conjecture.
Gavin McKab holds to the opinion that
Buckley will cut but a small figure in
the forthcoming campaign. He regards
the Bui key following as insignificant, be
lieving that the strength of the Buckley
clubs is overestimated. In his judgment
Buckley is simply working the wires to
get some delegates In the convention and
secure the nomination of a few Super-

HIT WITH A CRIB BOARD.
Ex-Justice of the Peace Carroll As-
sainted During a Game of
Euchre.
John A. Carroll, attorney and ex-Jus
tire of the Peacp, appeared in Judge Con
lan's court yesterday with a big lump
over his left eye. He told the Judge that !
he had been viciously assaulted the pre- I
vious r.iKht. and ho waninl h warrant fur
the arrest of his assailant on the charge
of batt. ry.
In answer to the Judge he said: "It
was this way. I was playing euchre in
Elmer's saloon, on Sacramento ,-i:i,i Jones
streets, last night. There were four of
us and one of my opponents was Edwin'
Fitzsimnicns. a gardener. My partner
passed and Fitzsimmons ordered him to !
turn the truiviu card down and he would |
H-.j it alone. J objected and Fitzsimmons
got mad. and grabbing hold of a cribbage
board sururk me over the eye. If it had
been an inch lower I would have lost the
sight of my eye."
The Judge instructed his warrant clerk
to issue the warrant, and yesterday after
noon Fltzsinimoiis was arrested. He was
released on giving JIV> cash bail.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, JUNE 22, 1599.
Oa/uLgtLt in a,n Eleotrioeil Storm.
The schooner W. S. Phelps, laden with gunpowder, dynamite and other explosives, was caught in three
thunder and lightning storms during the voyage from San Francisco to Champerico. While the lightning was
playing about the masts and rigging the officers and crew were in momentary anticipation of being blown to
atoms.
HAIR RAISING
EXPERIENCE OF
THE W. S. PHELPS
Lightning and Cargo
of Dynamite.
The crew of the schooner W. S. Phelps
had an experience that they never want
to duplicate during the voyage from San
Francisco to ''hamperico. The tight little
vessel went down in twenty-nine days,
a run that has not been equaled in years.
but that fact brings no consolation to
the sailors, and every time they think of
that voyage their hearts come up in their
mouths and their hair stands on end.
The W. S. Phelps was loaded with gun
powder, dynamite and other explosives.
During that run of twenty-nine days
three storms, accompanied by thunder
and lightning, were encountered; hut
while the wind watt fair the schooner
could never run away from the light
ning. It would burn on the, mastheads
like an electric li^ht and made a must
artistic display on the running rigging.
For a few moments v would be as light
as day and the next it would be as dark
as Erebus. Seas were constantly break
ing aboard and it was blowing a gale,
but the crew paid no attention to those
minor details. AVhat they watched with
breathless interest was the lightning and
each thunder dap was hailed with a sigh
of relief that the danger was temporarily
passed.
On an ordinary occasion Captain Nick
erson of the Fhelp.s would have t nought
nothing of the thunder and lightning
storms, but the accompanying cargo of
dynamite under his feet while the pyro
technic display was going on will make
him remember that Voyage as long as he
lives.
Thomas Gorman and Jerry Connor met
with a peculiar and painful accident on
the transport Sheridan yesterday. Both
are employes of the Fulton Iron Works
and were engaged on the repairs being
made on the transport's boilers. While
they were at work a man named Thomas
Murrey passed over the grating in the
'tween deck with a can of paraffine paint.
He stumbled and dropped the can. spilling
the contents Into the boiler room.
A considerable Quantity of the paint fell
(in Gorman, and on looking up and seeing
what had happened he got from under.
In doing so he stepped "ii a lighted candle
and that set the paint on his clothes
afire. Connor went to his assistance and
got burned in saving Gorman. Both men
were treated at the Harbor Hospital and
then Gorman was sent to St. Mary's Hos
pital, while Connor was s<>nt home. Gor
man was badly burned about the face,
head, neck and arms. <'onnor was burned
aboul the hands and wrists.
The. schooner Mary E. Foster arrived
from Honolulu yesterday. On June v Bhe
Bpoke the bark Rufus E. Wood in lati
tude 14 degrees 3S minutes north, longi
tude 137 degrees Â») minutes west. The
Wood is now out ninety-Six days from
Sydney. N. S. W.. for San Francisco, and
her owners are glad to hear from her.
The transport Zealandia will probably
get away to-day with four companies of
the Twenty-fourth Infantry and ISO re
cruits. The Conemaugh, which returned
from Manila last Tuesday, is to go back
with some of the horses of the Fourth
Cavalry.
William Turman, a carpenter, died in
the Harbor Hospital yesterday morning.
He was employed on Goat Island and had
a series of epileptic fits. He was hurried
to the city on the tug Vigilant, but died
soon after being admitted to tht hospital.
His widow lives in Salinas.

TRIAL OF O. M. WELBURN.
Government Closed Its Case Yester
day and the Defense Will
Be Taken Up.
The Government closed its rase in the
Welburn trial yesterday and the case for
the defense will be opened by Attorneys
McNab and Gould at 10 o'clock this morn
ing.
W. H. Dillard. the colored clerk, was
on the stand yesterday and admitted that
he signed Welpurn's mime to reports, etc..
by order of Air. Welburn. He said that
he had also signed the name of E. C.
Akins to checks on Welburn's order. He
knew that he was doing wrong at the
time, but he wanted to hold his position,
and he could do that only by obeying the
orders of his superior.
W. L. Zoller, formerly a. clerk under
Welburn, testified that his salary was at
the rate of $YX.*) per year. The vouchers
were made for larger amounts. He could
not tell what those amounts were, be
cause he hypothecated his salary every
nv'tith. George E. Dabovich gave similar
testimony as to his salary.
A misapprehension exists as to the tes
timony or Agent Thomas on Tuesday with
reference to his breaking open a desk in
\V. Iburn'B office. It was not Welburn's
desk. l>u! Louoe'S, and the letters "that
would not bear the light of day" were
Loupe's, not Welburn's, as stated.
New Dodge of Coolie Brokers.
The Secretary of the Treasury has ad
vised Collectors of the Port that Mexican
functionaries are uot authorized to issue
section 6 certificates to Chinese leaving
Mexico for the United States; that such
certificates are not to be recognized and
the holders of such are not entitled to
land. The decision was called forth bj
the efforts of Chinese brokers to land a
squad of Chinese laborers at Eagle Pass,
Tex., on the Mexican border.

MRS. STERETT
RECOVERS HER
LOST JEWELRY
Mysteriously Stolen
From Her Home.
The jewelry and silverware, valued at
, about $2000, which were supposed to have
been stolen from the residence of Mrs.
Emma Sterett, who figured as one of the |
! claimants to the estate of Gustav Wal
j ter, deceased, have been recovered by
j Detective Harper. They were traced to a
pawnshop at lOVz Stockton street, where
I they had been sold for about one-fifth their !
value. The supposed burglary occurred j
in August. WS. Mrs. Sterett had been |
visiting a friend, and on her return she
was amazed to find that her jewelry and
silverware had been taken during her ah- :
j sence. She at once notified the police, and i
Detective Harper was detailed to find the |
stolen property. After a short Investiga- !
tion he became convinced that the house
j had not been entered but that the jewelry
! and silverware had been taken by one of
the family.
By close inquiry the detective soon
I learned that the property had been
! pawned to Aaron and Leo Heringhi. who \
i then conducted a loan office at lo^ Stock- â 
i ton street. Harper told them that the j
jewelry was the property of Mrs. Sterett,
and warned them not to dispose of it \
until he had an opportunity to see her. j
A few days later Mrs. Sterett -visited the
i pawnshop and after identifying her jew- !
airy promised to return and redeem it.
On May 2 last Leo Heringhi, who has
since purchased his brother's Interest in
the pawnshop, sent Mrs. Sterett a writ- i
ten notice. Informing her that unless she \
redeemed the property, it would be sold
:at public auction. Not hearing from her i
| and concluding that she did not want
the jewelry, he sold it on May 17 at a
general sale of unredeemed pledges.
| On learning that her property had been
' disposed of Mrs. Sterett became indig- '
| nant. and at once hunted up Detective
; Harper, to whom she told the story of I
how the pawnbroker had sold her jew- i
elry. Harper lost no time in visiting
Heringhi' 8 place of business and pro- ;
ceeded to call him to account. Heringhi
explained that the articles had been in
pawn since the commission of the al
leged burglary, and that as no attempt
i had been made to redeem them he was '
i justified under the law in selling them to i
, the highest bidder. He readily gave the
j names of the persons who had bought the i
i stuff and, with the exception of a dia- '
i mono collar button the detective has
recovered all the stolen property. The
collar button was purchased by a man j
i named Livingston, who has promised to !
i turn it over to the police to-day.
Yesterday Mrs. Sterett visited lh'?|
I pawnshop and anxiously inquired about i
the diamond coilar button, which she
prizes very highly. She threatens
unless the article is promptly returned to
commence suit against the pawnbroker
for its recovery.
Heringhi. when seen last night, said j
that his brother had taken the jewelry in i
j pawn. He did not know that it was stolen I
i property, as he was given to understand
I by the person who "soaked" it that it
I belonged to a friend who was In need. I
\ He declared that after learning thnt the j
j jewelry was the property of Mrs. Sterett
j he communicated with her. advising her
! to redeem it or suffer having it sold at I
i public auction. Ap she ignored the notice
ho sold the stuff alrn.fr with a number of
other articles at auction.

HEALTH BOARD
SLATE STILL
IS UNBROKEN
Machete Falls With
Monotony.
The burden of this world's woes still
rests upon the hacks of Secretary Dunne
of the Board of Health and his follow
ers, who are seeking to enjoin their dis
missal. From last night's meeting of the
hoard, however, it Is manifest that they

under official, was accepted and a batch
of applications for positions placed on
file, as were a large number of instru
ments indorsing James P. Dockery. chief
I food inspector, the indorsements coming
from the most important business organi
zations, firms and corporations and many
prominent business men of the city, and a
petition asking that Miss Mary Patton be
retained in h*-r position as superintendent
of nurses. The matter of refusing to use
the Maclean hospital further as a pest
house also came up. and an order tr that
effect was made, and the application of a
nurse to be allowed to leave the institu
tion was also favorably acted imon. pro
viding that he will agree fo shave his
head and face alike and stand the fumi
gating process used to disinfect and kill
the germs of smallpox.
An application to allow the College of
Physicians and Surgeons the same num
ber of wards and beds and the same rep
resentation in the ranks of visiting physi
cians at the City and County Hospital
was also taken up and passed by the
hoard. Dr. Barhat being the only member
to vote against the application.
When the time for adjournment came it
was suggested that the board again meet
on Monday, but Dr. Kemey suggested
that an adjournment be taken to the call
lof the chair. "We want to see how good
Judge Hunt is going to be to us." said Dr.
Barbat, "before we call a meeting, and as
soon as he decides that the suit brought
by those win.) wish to retain 'heir posi
tions, notwithstanding the hoard, is with
out merit we will meet and resume our
labors." So .nded the meeting with the
slate unbroken.

Shrimp-Fishers Convicted.
The Fish Commissioners are rejoicing
over their victory in the conviction .of
four Chinese fishermen, who were charged
with violating the State fish law by hav
ing young fish, in their possession. The
Chinese were caught in San Pablo Bay
on May 26 with boats leaded with shrimps
and taken to San Pablo for trial before
Justice of the Peace Lindsay. Each of the
cases taken into the other counties result
ed in the jury failing to agree on a ver
dict. .

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND general
forecast:
The pressure has fallen slowly along the
coast of California, and has risen over Utah
and the Rocky Mountain region. The tem
perature has remain â¢'. nearly stationary over
the Pacific Slope. In the great va- eya of Call
fornia conditions arc favorable for warmer
weather with brisk northerly winds. A mod
erate norther will prevail in the valleys Thurs
day afternoon.
A maximum velocity of thirty-eight miles per
hour from the southwest is reported at El
Paso.
Conditions are favorable for fog Thursday :
ninrning and night along the coast of Cali
fornia.

NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK. June 21.â Transactions in stocks
were of paltry proportions, but this did not
preclude some pretty wide movements of
prices. In fact the lethargy of speculation left 1
the market vulnerable at any spot where genu
ine liquidation was thrown on it. Such liquida- .
tion was undoubtedly made on all of the
Tobacco stocks. American Tobacco suffered
most severely, the extreme decline being 8%.
Continental Tobacco dropped an extreme five
points and the preferred three. Very heavy
blocks of all three stocks running from 1000
to 500 i) shares changed hands In the course of
the decline or the hesitating rebound. The
break in these stocks was due to the rumors,
which have been persistent for several days,
of dissensions in the directory on a likelihood
of, a division of resources and possible de- |
structive competition. Coming on top of the
experience of the last few days in the steel
stocks to-day's events added largely to the
sentiment of disfavor held toward a large
number of industrial stocks. The steel stocks
themselves were pretty firm on account of the i
official assurance that the money for the pr<>- ,
ferred dividend on Federal St.- I had been Bet
aside before the declaration of the dividend
on the emmon, and also because of a preva
lent belief that Steel and Wire is soon to be
entered in the regular listed department of the i
Stock Exchange, which would necessitate a i ,
detailed statement of finances and earnings, j ,
American Smelting preferred showed a good
gain. The genera] market was semi-stagnant, '
but as a rule showed small losses.
Dealings In bonds were small, but prices j
were well held. Total sales, par value, $1,795,- [ '.

LONDON MARKET.
NEW YORK. June 21.â Commercial Ad
vertiser's London cablegram says:
The markets here were disturbed to-day by
flatness in Paris. They hardened finally on a
rumor that a new ministry had been formed.
Spanish fours were 60%@G1%. Tintos, 41%Â®43.
Consols fell to 107 11-16. closing at 107 15-16,
the lowest price this year. This is due to
dear money fears and the suspension of sink
ing fund purchases. Americans were quiet
and neglected, but New York caused a rally in
the afternoon by buying New York Central,
Northern Pacific and Union Pacific preferred.
