CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO, June 21.â Any hope among wheat
traders that yesterday's late steadiness was the
beginning of a reaction was dissipated before
the opening by ports of much-needed rains
in Russia, improved crop prospects in that
country and an early decline of %d In Liver
pool cables. The effect of this news on specu
lators was partly nullified at first by rain in
the Northwest, where too much moisture has
already put the wheat fields perilously near
the danger line, and after about fifteen minutes
of weakness the market developed strength on
covering by shorts. Opening sales of September
were made at 76^@76%c, compared with yes
terday's closing price of 78% c, and the liqui
dation that started at once carried the price
liown to 7- ,c. On this early break the South
west was a heavy seller, owing to the receipts
of new wheat. Covering by shorts, who saw
danger In the Northwest rains, carried tin
September price back to 76% c. but before 11
o'clock the market again developed extremo
weakness, and from that time until within a
few minutes of the close selling was persistent
and heavy. There was only a moderate cash
demand, while the seaboard reported light busi
ness with the other side, only 22 loads being
taken for export late in the day. Late in the
session, however, the depression was checked by
reports of continued heavy rains in the North
west and by some unfavorable advices from
Kansas. Late Russian advices were not so
favorable, but traders hail apparently lost their
Interest in the foreign situation and had turned
their attention to the domestic crop. Septem
ber declined to 75 1 ,i.fi 7">*se, but enough support
materialized in the last hour to cause a reac
tion to 76@76%c, where it closed.
Corn rympathized with wheat to some extent,
but the narrowness of trading prevented any
material decline. September closed a shade
lower.
Oats were dull but fairly steady. September
felt in a decree the weakness of wheat and |
closed a shade lower.
Provisions were almost at a standstill There
was a little selling; by grain people, but no
pressure from packers. At the close September
pork was unchanged, lard a shade lower and
ribs a shade higher.

BOSTON WOOL MARKET.
BOSTON, June 21â The American Wool and
Cotton Reporter will say to-morrow: The
wool market is stronger and more active. The
buying is broadening and Is participated In by
manufacturers, large and small, the specula
tors, traders and exporters. Foreigners are
a -a in in the market and have looked at some
lar^e lines of wool, including territories, and
it is understood, have purchased some at this
writing and are negotiating for others. The
demand continues to be plenty for territory
wools, nearly :).4^0.0C0 pounds having changed
hands. The country markets are stilf excited
and many of the largest and best lots of wool
in the interior have been corralled. Buyers
are "lining up" In Montana and there is every
indication that there will be a lively scramble
for the good clips and that every high prices
will have to be paid. The sales for the week
in Boston amounted to 5.500,400 pounds domestic
and 1.104.000 pounds foreign, making a total
of 6,604,400 pounds, against a ti.tal of 4,974.600
pounds for the previous week and 753,600 pounds
for the corresponding week last year. Sales
since January 1. 1599, amount to 118.524,200
pounds, against 53,643,810 pounds last year at
this time.

HAY AND FEEDSTUFFS.
The circular of Somera <fc Co. says of Hay:
"Although we are fairly well along into the
new season, yet matters continue very quiet,
and for some unaccountable reason the usual
June activity seems to be lacking. It is true,
however, that the Fourth of July is a turning
point with many consumers who refuse to buy
new crop hay previous to that date. Ship
ments of new hay have been quite free for sev
eral days past, and everything arriving now
seems to be in good condition. Judging from
what new hay has already arrived and from
reports received from many sections we are
convinced that the new crop is of very good
quality. Most of the new hay seems to be
quite fret from any foul matter, of just about
the right growth and of good color. These
facts alone should help establish a healthy
market here, although we can do nothing with
hay without a demand. The hay crop is a
heavy one throughout the country tributary to
San Francisco, and if our local market can
consume no more than last year we fear for
the future of the market. We learn however,
that the crops are very light down South, and
If they will hut. draw from the San Francisco
market it may help materially in establishing
fair prices later on. There Is a scarcity of
alfalfa at the present time and a fairly active
demand for same, therefore for new crop we
are able to quott; from $5 50 to $7 per ton. New
wheat hay, choice qualtiy, will bring about $10,
while the various good qualities of both wheat
and wheat and oat bring from $N to f.9. New
oat hay and certain qualities of wheat and
oat, more particularly the fine, short wire
bound goods, bring from $7 to $S. The prefer
ence is still given for old crop, although in
about two weeks' time we will see b<t little
discrimination. We are incline to believe, that
by that time consumers will b.> prepared to buy
largely of the new hay, which will enable the
market to run on a fairly steady basla."

NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given in the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day in the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day, the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day, except where there are but
three tides, as sometimes occurs. The heights
given are additions to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey rharts, except
wh*>n a minus sign (â ) precedes the height.
and then the number given la subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference is the mean of the lower low waters.

HAKLAND, the Hatchery of the Unexpected, furnished another
** mystery yesterday. A woman told the Chief of Police a
\ story of the abduction of a two-year-old boy that nas more
romantic than the oruinary fairy tale. After grabbing the child
1 the abductor drove off, and the woman, though 50 years old,
foiloweo him for four miles. The chase ended near the tidal
canal bridge, and when the abductor and his captive went into a
* secluded shanty the grandmother followed. The door was
ocked and she and the baby were kept prisoners for an hour,
% auring which time the abductor frequently threatened to kill her
? with a large knife. Watching her opportunity, she rushed out
k Hithjhe child and made her escape. Up to a late hour last
6 night the police had proved the truth of the grandmother's re
-9 markable story, and two officers were on guard all night watch
Â£ ing for the abductor's return. At 2 o'clock this morning he was
> arrested.
The woman is positive that the abductor was either drunk
* or crazy. There is now a belief that the man who stole the
* chiio is the same who attacked H. K. Belden with an ax at
* midnight two weeks ago.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call, ,
908 Broadway, June 21. ;
A remarkable story of abduction of a
two-year-old child was told to-day to
Chief of Police Fletcher by Mrs. M. Witt
holl and her daughter, Mrs. F. McGulre,
who live at 1421 Seventeenth street.
The acts of which the ladies told could |
only have been those of a madman, and
Chief Fletcher believes that no other
theory can possibly explain the remark
able incident.
August Walter is a grandson of Mrs.
Wlttholl and a nephew of Mrs. McGulre.
Mrs. Wittholl and the child were on the
â  beach at West Oakland yesterday after-
Tfnoon gathering shells for the chickens.
At Sixteenth and Wood streets they met
an unknown man in a buggy. He said
something in Knglish which Mrs. W'.tt
ho'il did not understand, as she is Ger
man and speaks English very imperfectly.
After a little conversation the man seized
the little grandson by the arm, placed
him in the buggy and drove off.
"The man seemed enraged that I did
not understand him," said Mrs. Wittholl,
"and he seized the child and was driving
away before 1 really knew what had.hap
pened. I followed the buggy and never
lost sight of it. After running what
seemed to me many miles the man turned
Into an open gateway on some street. lie
hitched his horse and carried the baby
Into the house. I was not far behind and
followed through the open door. 1 met
the nan lace to face, and when he saw
nit! is anger seemed to increase, He
grabbed a large knife and threatened to
kill me if 1 made any disturbance. He
went out of doors, where there was some
!:'<i;-e, and as ton as he was outside the
house 1 picked up the baby and ran from
the place before be returned. 1 could run
faster than he could, and got away. I !
do not know where we went, as 1 have
been here less than a year from St. Louis
and am not acquainted with the streets.
i am sure, however, that we crossed over
one bridge."
Mrs. Wittholl said she could easily iden.

with him for over an hour. He is aoout

sistance

Bhe had been held a prisoner for fully an

FIRED TWICE TO STOP
A FLEEING MAN
OFFICER SCOTT'S CHASE AFTER
A COLORED SOLDIER.
An Exciting Pursuit Near the Pre
sidio That Nearly Had a
Fatal Ending.
A member of the Twenty-fourth Tnfan
try. stationed at the Presidio, is thank
ful that his body is not perforated with
a bullet from the revolver of Officer Scott
of the North End station. On Tuesday
night the policeman lired'twice at the sol
dier, who was making frantic efftvrts to
avoid arr.-st, and this he succeeded in
doing by climbing over the Presidio fence
with greater force than ceremony, the
flight being hastened by the uncomfort
able hum of the leaden missile alined at
him.
1 hat the man was not killed is due to
his good fortune and not to the lack of
shooting ability (jf Officer Scott, who in
looked upon as one of the best shots on
the force. Shortly before the exciting
incideni a woman rushed up to the police
man and appealed for protection. She
breathlessly Informed him that a color. 1
man had instilled her while she was
walking along the street, and when she
remonstrated with him he picked up a
brick and struck her in the face, Indict
ing a severe wound. Her statement was
attest..! by the blood oozing out of a
deep gash on her cheek, and the officer,
quickly learning which way her assail
ant went, hurried in pursr.it. The un
known soldier, seeing the policeman,
broke into a run, and Snort started after
him. After repeatedly calling to him to
surrender, Scotr pulled out his revolver
and shot in the air to frighten his man,
but the report only had a tendency to in
crease his Bpeed. The pursued and pur
suer were close to the Presidio gate, but
this only redoubled Scott's desire to cap
ture his* man and he tired again with a
deliberate intention of winging him. But
for the darkness he might have suc
ceeded. As it was the negro went pell

sentry, who was aroused by the shooting.
Scott, finding himself defeated, hurried
back to the spot where he left the
woman, but was disappointed, as she had

lice Lees last night in which he narrated

affair to the olerk in charge. She ex
pressed a desire that her name be not
mentioned in the affair, as ?he disliked
notoriety. A formal charge will be
lodged with the military authorities to

punished for his action. It is understood

WOULD BE UP TO DATE.
Invitation Committee Wishes to Do
the Grand on the Fourth.
The first signs of dissension are showing
themselves In the Fourth of July Commit
tee. The invitation committee has taken
What it is pleased to call a "stand" on the
matter of invitations, and its members
threaten resignations and other horrible
things if they are not allowed to have
their own way. They want to issue en
graved and embossed invitations, and the
general committee is willing to be satis
fied with common printed ones. En
graved invitations will cost $12 a hun
dred; printed ones will cost about a sixth
as much, but still the invitation commit
tee is determined to be up to date, 'and
the question has come to a head on that
point. It will he fought out before the
meeting of the executive committee this
evening.
Eustace fitllinane, a young newspaper
man of this city, lias been select e,< t.,
write the poem for '.he celebration. Ed
win Marknam, the author of the "Man
With a Hoe," volunteered to write the
poem for the day. but as pressing F.ast
i in engagements would prevent him deliv
ering It in person, he has decided to write
a memorial to the her tea of the late war.
"The Old Flag Forever," an epic written
by Mrs. LellahFrance McDermott, will be
played by the orchestra at the literary ex
ercises and a grand chorus will sing the
words of the stirring melody.
The arrival of the Philadelphia assures
a large contingent of men-of-warsmen in
the. parade. An invitation to participate
has been extended to Admiral Kautz ami
his men, and it is expected the blue
jackets who made themselves famous at
Samoa a few weeks ago will be out in
force.
Daniel Norcross, the pioneer grand mar
shal, who was In charge of the parade in
this city in 1*49, has accepted the Invita
tion of the grand nvirshal to act In an
official capacity In the coming celebration.

LAID ASIDE THEIR
PUNCHES FOR PLEASURE
CONDUCTORS ENJOYED A MUCH
NEEDED OUTING.
An Ideal Day and a Picked Crowd
Made Their Initial Picnic a Bril
liant Success â Who Were
Present.
There was a dearth of railway conduc
tors on the various divisions of this State
yesterday. Every ticket puncher who
could get away from his duties without
danger of tying up his line gathered his
family and capacious lunch basket to
gether and hied himself to Sunset Park
to attend the initial outing- of the mem
bers of his calling: given under the aus
pices of El Capitan Division No. 115. The
Knights of the Punch sat back in up
holstered cars, through which it is their
wont to pass in the collection of paste
boards, and calmly handed their printed
slips to' one of their tribe, and half pitied
the latter fur being compelled to work
on such an auspicious day.
If there was ever a success achieved
by an organization on its first venture
in the picnic line, the railroad conductors
are entitled to their share of the laurels.
Their picnic proved one of the most de
lightful held in Sunset Park, or, in fact,
any other park, in some moons. A per
fect day, a select crowd and not a hitch
in the arrangements made the outing a
memorable one.
Representatives from the surrounding
bay cities were there in numbers, garbed
in their coolest apparel, laden with the
good things that make a picnic pleasant
and without a care but to have a good
time. And they had it. too. If a crowded
dance hall is a criterion and if beaming
faces and merry laughter in other por
tions of the beautiful park are signifi
cant.
Fourteen cars carried the conductors,
their families and their numerous friends
to the mountains. A start was made at
8:45 a. m., and the heavily loaded train
stopped at rarious points along the route
to take on more. Near Mount Eden the
cylinder blew out of the locomotive and
caused a trifling delay. With a series of
jerks and jolts the engine was started
and hurried on to San Jose without fur
ther mishap, but there gave way to an
undamaged locomotive. The picnickers
arrived at Sunset Park shortly after noon
and immediately spread themselves about
the grounds and feasted on the good
things stored In their capacious hampers.
After, luncheon dancing was inaugurated,
and until 5 o'clock Richardson's Sausa
lito band discoursed popular music for
the graceful ones. Strolls down by the
babbling brook that courses through the
grounds were taken by numerous parties
and along its banks were children and
grown people dangling their "Trilbies"
in its cool current.
Among the familiar faces in and around
the dance hall during the day's festivities
were genial Sam Davis, one of the nar
row gauge's oldest conductors; C. D.
Phelps, A. R. Murray, J. D. Moffltt. J. C.
Frelling, George ]Â Colegrove, W. D.
Hartley. A. H. Hoibrook and W. H. Eng
wicht, of the Southern Pacific Company;
W. J. Hunter and J. K. Smith of the Cali
fornia and Northwestern.
W. J. Hunter made an efficient floor
manager and the other members of the
committee of arrangements, consisting of
Thomas Billingslea, Charles D. Phelps, J.
E. Dillon, Oeorgr L. Colegrove and
George K. Smith, also did valiant service
in making the affair a brilliant success.
An instance of the thoroughness In
which the railroad men conducted their
outing was shown on tho return journey,
when it was learned by telegraph that
twenty-three picnickers were left on the
grounds, through no fault of the com
mittee, but due to the twenty-three being
in the canyon when the signal was given
to depart. Without a minute's, hesitation
the committee ordered a special end: 1 ,"
and car to the park and the belated pic
nickers were whirled hr>me In style. Such
courtesy won further words "f aj>>proval
for the conductors and was a fitting close
to a jolly outing.

AFTERMATH OF A DANCE.
A Gang of Toughs Handle Police
Officers Roughly.
Shortly before midnight Officer Riordan
arrested two toughs who were fighting
at Sixth and Minna streets. He started
for a call box, when he was assaulted by-
John Barry. Releasing the two fighters
he started after his assailant and cap
tured him. Officer Minnehan, who came
to Rlordan's assistance, caught one of the
men released by him, but was aHacked by
the other tough, who broke Minnehan' B
nose and inflicted a scalp wound. The of
ficer wan taken to the Receiving Hospital.
The toughs originally arrested escaped.
Harry was charged with having used vul
gar language, battery and resisting an of
licer. Another of the gang. Joseph Mur
phy, was also arrested and bonked with
having used vulgar language. The pugilis
tic crowd had been attending a dance in
the vicinity.
CITY CREDITORS ANXIOUS.
Debts Incurred in Violation of the
One-Twelfth Act in Jeopardy.
The Auditor will hold a conference
with the Finance Committee of the
Board of Supervisors in a few days In
regard to the payment of certain claims
that have been incurred in violation of
the one-twelfth act.
The former board left bills unpaid to
the amount of $20,773 04, including street
labor claims for the month of December
last, which have heen Ignored on the
ground that they were hot Incurred by
the presr-nt administration. Since Janu
ary 1 b'lls amounting to $122,300 have been
incurred and remain unpaid. If these are
not liquidated before the 30th lnst., un
der the provisions of the law they be
come void.
Several business firms having claims
I against the city are. preparing to take
! legal steps to secure their claims. It is
t generally conceded that the deficiency will
i amount to nearly Slfti.OOn. and the salaries
of the statutory officers may be held up
to obtain necessary funds to satisfy the
City a creditors. It is understood that the
Auditor will propose as a solution of the
difficulty that the one-twelfth act be sus
pended and the claims be immediately
paid.

OIL COMPANY BLAMED
FOR HOLBROOK'S DEATH
BUSINESS MEN RENDER A VER-
DICT OF CENSURE.
Tanks Should Not Be Tapped After
Dark When Lamplight Was
Necessarily Obliged to
Be Used.
An inquest was held yesterday morn
ing by Coroner Hill on the body of
Charles Hoibrook, who came to his death
last Tuesday morning from injuries re
ceived by an explosion of coal oil in the
i works of the Union Oil Company. The
| Jury, composed of business men, censured
the oil company and also the draylng
company for which Hoibrook had been
working.
The testimony on behalf of the oil com
pany was that Hoibrook called at Ihe
works for a load of oil after dark, and !
that the man in charge of the works tit
that hour had his pood clothes on, and
mentioned that fact to Hoibrook. who i
volunteered to fill the package himself, i
He was given a lantern, which he set by'
the Bide of the tank. Some of the oil
bubbled and sputtered upon the lantorn,
settiriK fire to Hoibrook and the tank.
The agents of the company contende-i
at the inquest that it was no part of Hoi
brook's duty to decant the oil. and taat
he alone was to blame, he having volun
teered his services. It appeared. a!.-v>,
that the oil company did not employ a
watchman. The jury returned a verdict
as follows:
That the said Charles Holbrook, a*ed 47.
nativity Massachusetts, occupation teamster,
resilience 334.3 Twenty-fourth street, in the city
anil county of San Francisco, came to his
death at the City Receiving Hospital In said
city and county on the 20th day of June, IK'.t9,
from ihock following burns; and we do further
find the Union Oil Company criminally negli
gent In so far as to allow oil to be taken from
Its premises at a time when lamplight had
to be used. And we further find the Bocarde
Drayage Company negligent In allowing its
teamster to perform the duty that caused his
death.

jury:

FELL FROM A WINDOW.
Mrs. Schneider's Lottery Ticket May
Cost Her Her Life.
Mrs. Louisa Schneider, a widow living
at -125 Bush street, fell from a second
story window last nigbl and sustained in
juries from which she may not recover.
Mrs. Schneider held a winning ticket in
the lottery and cashed it in yesterday
morning. She then si.Tted out to have a
irood time with the money, and night
found her in a condition bordering on in
toxication. At about tj o'clock she went to
the window to attend to some flowers on v
shelf, iost her balance and fell to the hard
pavement of the alleyway in low.
She was picked up unconscious and
taken to the Harbor Receiving Hospital,
where it was ascertained that she had
sustained a fracture of the right thigh,
with probably internal injuries.
Socialists Complain of the Police.
The meeting of the Socialist Labor
party on the corner of Sixth and Market
streets was broken up last evening by the
1 police arid the members of the party com
plain bitterly of the way in which it was
done. They say their speaker was pulled
i down from the stand with unnecessary
brutality anil that language unfit for
| repetition was used. The speaker was
Arthur McQinty; he was taken to the
; Centra] Station and locked up on a charge
of obstructing the sidewalk. When tne
I organizer of the party, Thomas Beres
ford, produced $2n bail, The customary
amount in such cases, he was told the
bail was $100. He was not told who fixed
it at that amount, but, as he says, he was
hustled out of the prison and McGinty
was locked up.
Lumbermen Organize.
A mass-meetingr of lumbermen was held
in the hall of the Coast Seamen's Union
at East and Mission streets last night for
' the purpose of considering the advisabil
| ity of forming a union. About three hun
| dred men were present and they listened
I with interest to addresses on organization
by A. R. Andre and Andrew Furuscth, or
ganizing committee of the San Francisco
Labor Council. A temporary organiza
tion was formed and Mose Kuehn elected
treasurer and Frank Butler secretary. I.
Donelly was appointed to act with the
| two officers as a committee of managers
until the next meeting, which will be held
to-morrow night. The majority of those
I present signed the roll.

Roche's Judgment Confirmed.
The Supreme Court yesterday handed
down a decision affirming the judgment
of the lower court in the case of Thomas
Roche against W. P. Redington et al.
Roche was run over and severely Injured
by a horse and cart belonging to defend
ant and brought suit for damages. He
was awarded $4000 and Redington took an
appeal to the Supreme Court.

BUNKO MAN CAUGHT.
He Fleeced a Colorado Man With the
Lock Game.
T. G. Dobyns en me from Colorado a few
days ago and fell in with a brace of lock
pame workers. They took him to see the
sights of Chinatown last Tuesday night
and got him to bet $4 that he could open
a lock after they had shown him how to
do it. He lost his money, for they had
changed the lock for one that a black
smith could not open with a sledgeham
mer. Yesterday, seeing one oi the men,
Joseph Brown, on California street, he
had him .arrested. The two locks were
found on Brown.

HENRY T. OXNARD, Who Champions the Beet Sugar Industry
ROBBED MAIL BOXES
FOR AN EASY LIVING
Eugene Franks Boldly Admits
That for Years He Has
Followed This Practice.
EUGENE FRANKS, "â¢>â  - was arrested Tuesday night by Detectives
Dinan and Reynolds, on Bush street, for stealing packages from the
top of .mail boxes, will have to answer to four charges of petty lar
ceny. The postal authorities cannot prosecute him, as the packages were
:iot within the mail boxes.
Franks is a man "2 years cf age and is one of the class Imbued with
the idea that it is undignified to w<;rk for a living as long as it can be done
by stealing. For several years he has made it a practice to steal packages
from the top of mall boxes, and ha* lived by pawning the articles contained
in the stolen packages. About two years ago the postal authorities received
complaints that packages martod had not reached their destinations, and
iho police were notified. Dinan ftÃd Reynolds were at that time detailed on
the case, and they arrested Fi"Â»nks in the commission of the offense. He
was sentenced to six months in the County Jail.
Recently complaints were again received by the postal authorities and the
police were again notified. Dtnan and Reynolds suspected Franks, and they
shadowed him and succeeded in arresting him Wednesday night, while he
was in the act of taking packages from the top of a mail box on Bush
street. Yesterday they went to his room in a lodging-house on Bush street
and Becused a large number of packages that had been stolen and opened.
They were taken to the City Prison, and Postal Inspector Erwln was notified
and obtained a list of the addresses on the packages.
Franks was seen in the City Prison yesterday and frankly admitted that
he stole the packages, and was willing to assist the police and postal authori
ties in recovering the articles he had stolen from the packages and pawned.
He said he had no excuse to offer 'or his conduct, only that it Mas an easy
way to get a living.
The fault of leaving men like Franks open to the temptation of stealing
these packages does not lie with the postal authorities, but with boys em
ployed in stores, as a rule. They are ordered by tYieir employers to post tho
packages in the general postoffice or one of the branch offices, but instead of
doing so place them on top of the nearest mail bo* -and leave them openly
there to be removed by any one who has a desire ti> do so.
tack on the tariff in general, and the
home sugar industry in particular, may
be accounted for by his desire to dlrsct
the attention away from trusts and have
it centered on the tariff. The experiences
of depression following the Wilson act,
contrasted with the prosperity which BUC
ceeded the McKinley law, will hardly fool
the majority of the American people. Mr.
Havemeyer and others having selfish in
u-r.-sis to protect notwithstanding. Mr.
Havemeyer wants raw sugar free, and
favors Cuban planters against American
farmers. This might be an additional rea
son for his suggestion that Cuban sugar
should be admitted free, but the main
and, in my opinion, underlying reason for
his statement rests on the fact that he
sees the final destruction of the sugar re
fining Industry Threatened by the eomiit?
prosperity of the home beet sugar inaus
try. which is beginning to assume moat
important proportions, and which exists
to-day in Caifornia, Oregon, Washington.
Utah. New Mexico. Colorado, Nebraska,
Minnesota, Wisconsin, Illinois. Michigan
;ir;d New York, having developed phe
nomenally'in tho last two years.
There is no trust among the sugar pro
ducers of this country, and there never
can be, for the simple reason that the
beet sugar industry can be started in al
most any part of tlif Union.
I conclude by calling attention to the
fact that the home sugar Industry, which
Mr. Havemeyer claims to bo inordinately
protected, is not in a trust, whereas the
sugar refining industry, which he claims
does not receive oni-half of the protec
tion it requires. Is, -vfith the Standard Oil
Company, the pioneer of this new busi
ness consolidation known as trust.
T will not dispute Mr. Havemeyor's
claim that 10 per cent is sufficient profec-
Tinn to the sugar refining Interests which
he represents, but I do assert that he can

MANY MERRYMAKERS
AT SHELL MOUND PARK
STAR OF THE SEA CHURCH
GIVES A PICNIC.
Father Coyle's Parishioners Have a
Jolly Timeâ Fat Men's Race
Excites Unusual Interest.
The sixth annual picnic of the Star of
the Sea Parish at Shell Mound Park yes
terday was a grand success. Over 1000
' merrymakers journeyed across the bay to
the pleasure grounds, and there was not
one unpleasant incident to mar the enjoy
ment of the occasion.
It was a family picnic, and there wero
I many little ones present who were made
i happy by all kinds of amusements. There
were baskets of good things in profusion,
and after full iustice had been done to
| them dancing:, under the direction of
Musician Blanchard, was enjoyed.
At 2 o'clock the racing events, for valu
able prizes, were carried out under the
personal direction of Rev. Father J. P.
Coyle, pastor of the church. The fat
men's race excited the most interest.
There were four contestants: Officer Hen
ry Pyle, 210 pounds; Customs Inspector W.
P. Johnson, 209; Insurance Agent Harry
Kelly. 180, and Capitalist "Billy" Curttn,
185. In the vernacular, "there was noth- I
ing to it," for Johnson led all the- way and
came in laughing, with Pyle a good sec
ond. Kelly looked as if he had walked
around the track, and Curtin was "abso
lutely last."

Hands.
Men walking the streets of the city beg
ging for an opportunity to work or ths
price of a meal and a place to sleep.
Farmers frantic over the prospect of los
ing their crops, representing time, labor
and money expended, because help is
scarceâ almost impossible to secure at any
price.
Such is the peculiar condition of the la
bor market to-day. The laws of supply
and demand seem to have slipped a cog,
thereby stopping the machinery which
moves the prosperity of the State. A
number of prominent land owners holding
properties in several parts of California
have been obliged to send to the city for
Chinese and Japanese help to harvest
their crops because they were unable to
get white men at any reasonable price.
One of them yesterday in discussing the
situation stated that never in his recollec
tion has there been such a scarcity of
workir.gmen in the country as at the pres
ent time. From San Diego to the far
north the same complaint has been made,
and to-day hundreds of orders for work
lngmen are on the books of the employ
ment offices in this city. F. W. Zeile,
president of the Mercantile Trust Com
pany, had his attention called to the situ
ation, and in discussing the subject yes
terday said: "Within the last few weeks
1 have met a number of landowners from
all parts of the State, and they are com
plaining bitterly because they are unable
to secure help to get in their crops. These
farmers do not represent any particular
locality, but come from the Sacramento
and San Joanuin valleys, from the Salinas
and the Santa Clara districts.
"In the big valleys there is hay and
grain to harvest, and in the smaller \ al
leys the orchardists are beginning to feel
the necessity of having a few additional
men to help handle the fruit crop. From
what 1 caji learn, all kinds of good wages
are offered. The common hay and
handler can get from SI 25 to $2 50 per day
and found. The more expert harvesters
can earn more. Order after order has
been sent to the employment offices here,
and still they remain unfilled. There has
been some talk to the effect that the ia
boring men in the city have not the means
to reach tho farms and that is what is
keeping them from going into the country.
Whatever may be the reason, the fact re
mains the same â the farmers are sadly in
need of help and hundreds of irile men are
to be seen around the city every day ask
ing for work."
Inquiry proves that the lack of means to
get to* the places where work is to be ob
tained is not the reason for the idle men
remaining in the cities. It has always
been the custom for the employer to ad
vance the necessary money for the sturdy
worker to get to the country, the amount
of his f.-re bNng dfduetod from the first
money he earns. Whatever may be tine
reason for the worker and tho farmer not
coming together, the fact remains the
sampâ there is plenty of work to be had
In the. country and the farmers are
ohl'gc d to fill th^ places of vrhlte men with
Asiatics. It has been stated that at the
present time the country is overrun with
men seeking work. A reputable gentle
man owning several farms in various
parts of the State asserted that nearly all
who have comn to his places are. nothing
but tramps, who are thoroughly unrelia
ble and worso than useless in any work
at which they can be put. These are the
fellows who are believed to be responstnl*
for keeping many worthy men away from
the country by spreading the statement
that work Is scarce; and "wages low. The
"hobo" is worthless at any price.

McSweeney Hovel Burned.
The. filthy hovel at 12fi Tltah street,
i which up to within the last few days fur
nished a refuge for the aged McSweeney
sisters, is no more. The building, to
gether with everything it contained, was
burned as a sanitary precaution by order
of Dr. Lawler, the Hralth Officer, yeeter
day, with the free consent of the owner of
the property.
